crew used the shower after exercise and found it a positive experience. .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shayler_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shayler-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cameras_and_film">Cameras and film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Cameras and film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="2370" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/170px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/255px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/340px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of the Skylab space station taken with a hand-held 70 mm Hasselblad camera using a 100 mm lens and SO-368 medium speed Ektachrome film</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4248" data-file-width="4318" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/220px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/330px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/440px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> as photographed on June 22, 1973 from Skylab</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:40_Years_Ago,_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1235" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/220px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/330px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/440px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:40_Years_Ago,_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skylab as SL2 mission departs</div></div></div>\n<p>There was a variety of hand-held and fixed experiments that used various types of film. In addition to the instruments in the ATM solar observatory, 35 and 70&#160;mm film cameras were carried on board. A TV camera was carried that recorded video electronically. These electronic signals could be recorded to magnetic tape or be transmitted to Earth by radio signal. The TV camera was not a digital camera of the type that became common in the later decades, although Skylab did have a digital computer using microchips onboard.\n</p><p>It was determined that film would fog up to due to radiation over the course of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-69">[69]</a></sup> To prevent this film was stored in vaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_69-5"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Personal (hand-held) camera equipment:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Television camera\n<ul><li>Westinghouse color</li>\n<li>25&#8211;150&#160;mm zoom</li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/16_mm_film" title="16 mm film">16 mm film</a> video camera (Maurer), called the 16&#160;mm Data Acquisition Camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-90">[90]</a></sup> The DAC was capable of very low frame rates, such as for engineering data films, and it had independent shutter speeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-91">[91]</a></sup> It could be powered from a battery or from Skylab itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-91">[91]</a></sup> It used interchangeable lenses, and various lens and also film types were used during the missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-91">[91]</a></sup>\n<ul><li>There were different options for <a href="/wiki/Frame_rate" title="Frame rate">frame rates</a>: 2, 4, 6, 12 and 24 frames per second<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Lenses available: 5, 10, 18, 25, 75, and 100&#160;mm</li>\n<li>Films used:\n<ul><li>Ektachrome film</li>\n<li>SO-368 film</li>\n<li>SO-168 film</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>Film for the DAC was contained in DAC Film Magazines, which contained up to 140 feet (42.7 m) of film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> At 24 frames per second this was enough for 4 minutes of filming, with progressively longer film times with lower frame rates such as 16 minutes at 6 frames per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-91">[91]</a></sup> The film had to be loaded or unloaded from the DAC in a photographic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_room" title="Dark room">dark room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>35&#160;mm film cameras (Nikon)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-94">[94]</a></sup>\n<ul><li>There were 5 Nikon 35&#160;mm film cameras on board, with 55&#160;mm and 300&#160;mm lenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>\n<li>They were specially modified <a href="/wiki/Nikon_F" title="Nikon F">Nikon F</a> cameras<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imaging.nikon.com_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-imaging.nikon.com-96">[96]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The cameras were capable of interchangeable lenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imaging.nikon.com_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-imaging.nikon.com-96">[96]</a></sup></li>\n<li>35mm films included:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ektachrome" title="Ektachrome">Ektachrome</a></li>\n<li>SO-368,</li>\n<li>SO-168</li>\n<li>2485 type film</li>\n<li>2443 type film</li></ul></li></ul></li>\n<li>70&#160;mm film camera (Hasselblad)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-90">[90]</a></sup>\n<ul><li>This had an electric data camera system with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reseau_plate" title="Reseau plate">Reseau plate</a></li>\n<li>Films included\n<ul><li>70&#160;mm Ektachrome</li>\n<li>SO-368 film</li></ul></li>\n<li>Lenses: 70&#160;mm lens, 100&#160;mm lens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>Experiment S190B was the Actron Earth Terrain Camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The S190A was the <i>Multispectral Photographic Camera </i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_90-4"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>This consisted of six 70&#160;mm cameras</li>\n<li>Each was an Itek 70&#160;mm boresighted camera</li>\n<li>Lenses were f/2.8 with a 21.2 degree <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>There was also a pair of <a href="/wiki/Esselte_Leitz_GmbH_%26_Co_KG" title="Esselte Leitz GmbH &amp; Co KG">Leitz</a> Trinovid 10 x 40 binoculars modified for use in space to aid in Earth observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computers">Computers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Computers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2224" data-file-width="1795" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c2/Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg/220px-Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c2/Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg/330px-Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c2/Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg/440px-Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasasoftwareforskylabcycle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Computational cycle of the Skylab computer program</div></div></div>\n<p>Skylab was controlled in part by a digital computer system, and one of its main jobs was to control the pointing of the station; pointing was especially important for its solar power collection and observatory functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup> The computer consisted of two actual computers, a primary and a secondary. The system ran several thousand words of code, which was also backed up on the Memory Load Unit (MLU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup> The two computers were linked to each other and various input and output items by the workshop computer interface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Operations could be switched from the primary to the backup, which were the same design, either automatically if errors were detected, by the Skylab crew, or from the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Skylab computer was a space-hardened and customized version of the TC-1 computer, a version of the <a href="/wiki/IBM_System/4_Pi" title="IBM System/4 Pi">IBM System/4 Pi</a>, itself based on the <a href="/wiki/IBM_System/360" title="IBM System/360">System 360</a> computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup>  The TC-1 had a 16,000 word memory based on ferrite memory cores, while the MLU was a read-only <a href="/wiki/Tape_drive" title="Tape drive">tape drive</a> that contained a backup of the main computer programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup>  The tape drive would take 11 seconds to upload the backup of the software program to a main computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardware_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardware-100">[100]</a></sup> The TC-1 used 16-bit words and the <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processor</a> came from the 4Pi computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardware_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hardware-100">[100]</a></sup> There was a 16k and an 8k version of the software program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Software_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Software-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The computer weighed 100 pounds (45.4&#160;kg, 7.14 stones) and consumed about ten percent of the station\'s <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electrical power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-5"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Apollo Telescope Mount Digital Computer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardware_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hardware-100">[100]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Attitude and Pointing Control System (APCS)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-6"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Memory Load Unit (MLU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computer_98-7"><a href="#cite_note-computer-98">[98]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>After launch the computer is what the controllers on the ground communicated with to control the station\'s orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ch3-1_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ch3-1-103">[103]</a></sup> When the sun-shield was torn off the ground staff had to balance solar heating with electrical production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ch3-1_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ch3-1-103">[103]</a></sup> On March 6, 1978 the computer system was re-activated by NASA to control the re-entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reactivation_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reactivation-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The system had a user interface which consisted of a display, ten buttons, and a three position switch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-User_Interfaces_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-User_Interfaces-105">[105]</a></sup> Because the numbers were in <a href="/wiki/Octal" title="Octal">octal</a> (base-8), it only had numbers zero to seven (8 keys), and the other two keys were enter and clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-User_Interfaces_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-User_Interfaces-105">[105]</a></sup> The display could show minutes and seconds which would count-down to orbital benchmarks, or it could display keystrokes when using the interface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-User_Interfaces_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-User_Interfaces-105">[105]</a></sup> The interface could be used to change the software program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-User_Interfaces_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-User_Interfaces-105">[105]</a></sup> The user interface was called the Digital Address System (DAS) and could send commands to the computer\'s command system. The command system could also get commands from the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Software_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Software-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For personal computing needs Skylab crews were equipped with models of the then new hand-held electronic scientific calculator, which was used in place of slide-rules used on prior space missions as the primary personal computer. The model used was the <a href="/wiki/HP-35" title="HP-35">Hewlett Packard HP 35</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Some slide rules continued in use aboard Skylab, and a <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule" title="Slide rule">circular slide rule</a> was at the workstation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans_for_re-use_after_the_last_mission">Plans for re-use after the last mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Plans for re-use after the last mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SkylabRescue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="2363" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/170px-SkylabRescue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/255px-SkylabRescue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/340px-SkylabRescue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SkylabRescue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skylab Rescue vehicle Apollo CSM being removed from its <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> rocket after the last Skylab misson</div></div></div>\n<p>The three crewed Skylab missions used only about 16.8 of the 24 man-months of oxygen, food, water, and other supplies stored aboard Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> A fourth manned mission was under consideration, which would have used the launch vehicle kept on standby for the Skylab Rescue mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com-109">[109]</a></sup> This would have been a 20-day mission to boost Skylab to a higher altitude and do more scientific experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com-109">[109]</a></sup>  Another plan was to use a <a href="/wiki/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System" title="Teleoperator Retrieval System">Teleoperator Retrieval System</a> (TRS) launched aboard the Space Shuttle (then under development), to robotically re-boost the orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-110">[110]</a></sup> When Skylab 5 was cancelled, it was expected Skylab would stay in orbit until the 1980s, which was enough time to overlap with the beginning of Shuttle launches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-110">[110]</a></sup> Other options for launching TRS included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_III" title="Titan III">Titan III</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_Agena" title="Atlas Agena">Atlas Agena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-110">[110]</a></sup> None of these options received the level of effort and funding needed for execution before Skylab\'s sooner-than-expected re-entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-110">[110]</a></sup> Skylab\'s internal systems were evaluated and tested from the ground, and effort was put into plans for re-using it, as late as 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though no one returned after the end of the SL-4 mission in February 1974, the crew left a bag filled with supplies to welcome visitors, and left the hatch unlocked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup> NASA discouraged any discussion of additional visits due to the station\'s age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> but in 1977 and 1978, when the agency still believed the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> would be ready by 1979, it completed two studies on reusing the station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> By September 1978, the agency believed Skylab was safe for crews, with all major systems intact and operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> It still had 180 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man-days" title="Man-days">man-days</a> of water and 420 man-days of oxygen, and astronauts could refill both;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup> the station could hold up to about 600 to 700 man-days of drinkable water and 420 man-days of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Before SL-4 left they did one more boost, running the Skylab thrusters for 3 minutes which added 11&#160;km in height to its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-117">[117]</a></sup> Skylab was left in a 433 by 455&#160;km orbit on departure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-117">[117]</a></sup> At this time, the NASA-accepted estimate for its re-entry was nine years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>..Calculations made during the mission, based on current values for solar activity and expected atmospheric density, gave the workshop just over nine years in orbit. Slowly at first-dropping 30 kilometers by 1980-and then faster-another 100 kilometers by the end of 1982-Skylab would come down, and some time around March 1983 it would burn up in the dense atmosphere.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite><i>SP-4208 Living and Working In Space: A History of Skylab</i>, Chapter 19<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-117">[117]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The studies cited several benefits from reusing Skylab, which one called a resource worth "hundreds of millions of dollars"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-118">[118]</a></sup> with "unique habitability provisions for long duration space flight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-119">[119]</a></sup> Because no more operational Saturn V rockets were available after the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, four to five shuttle flights and extensive <a href="/wiki/Space_architecture" title="Space architecture">space architecture</a> would have been needed to build another station as large as Skylab\'s 12,400 cubic feet (350&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Its ample size&#8212;much greater than that of the shuttle alone, or even the shuttle plus <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>&#8212;was enough, with some modifications, for up to seven astronauts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> of both sexes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and experiments needing a long duration in space;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-118">[118]</a></sup> even a movie projector for recreation was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Proponents of Skylab\'s reuse also said repairing and upgrading Skylab would provide information on the results of long-duration exposure to space for future stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup> The most serious issue for reactivation was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationkeeping" title="Stationkeeping">stationkeeping</a>, as one of the station\'s <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> had failed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson361_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-benson361-124">[124]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_control_system" title="Attitude control system">attitude control system</a> needed refueling; these issues would need EVA to fix or replace. The station had not been designed for extensive resupply. However, although it was originally planned that Skylab crews would only perform limited maintenance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> they successfully made major repairs during EVA, such as the SL-2 crew\'s deployment of the solar panel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and the SL-4 crew\'s repair of the primary coolant loop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The SL-2 crew fixed one item during EVA by, reportedly, "hit[ting] it with [a] hammer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some studies also said, beyond the opportunity for space construction and maintenance experience, reactivating the station would free up shuttle flights for other uses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-118">[118]</a></sup> and reduce the need to <a href="/wiki/Extended_Duration_Orbiter" title="Extended Duration Orbiter">modify the shuttle for long-duration missions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Even if the station were not manned again, went one argument, it might serve as an experimental platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shuttle_mission_plans">Shuttle mission plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Shuttle mission plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3552" data-file-width="4620" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/220px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/330px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/440px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Concept for proposed Skylab re-boost</div></div></div>\n<p>The reactivation would likely have occurred in four phases:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Canceled_Space_Shuttle_missions#STS-2A_(Columbia)" title="Canceled Space Shuttle missions">An early Space Shuttle flight</a> would have boosted Skylab to a higher orbit, adding five years of operational life. The shuttle might have pushed or towed the station, but attaching a booster&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System" title="Teleoperator Retrieval System">Teleoperator Retrieval System</a> (TRS)&#8212;to the station would have been more likely, based on astronauts\' training for the task. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta" title="Martin Marietta">Martin Marietta</a> won the contract for $26 million to design the apparatus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790101_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-time19790101-133">[133]</a></sup> TRS would contain about three tons of propellant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The remote-controlled booster had TV cameras and was designed for duties such as space construction and servicing and retrieving satellites the shuttle could not reach. After rescuing Skylab, the TRS would have remained in orbit for future use. Alternatively, it could have been used to de-orbit Skylab for a safe, controlled re-entry and destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dempewolff197808_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-dempewolff197808-135">[135]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In two shuttle flights, Skylab would have been refurbished. In January 1982, the first mission would have attached a docking adapter and conducted repairs. In August 1983, a second crew would have replaced several system components.</li>\n<li>In March 1984, shuttle crews would have attached a solar-powered Power Expansion Package, refurbished scientific equipment, and conducted 30- to 90-day missions using the Apollo Telescope Mount and the earth resources experiments.</li>\n<li>Over five years, Skylab would have been expanded to accommodate six to eight astronauts, with a new large docking/interface module, additional logistics modules, Spacelab modules and pallets, and an orbital vehicle space dock using the shuttle\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_tank" title="External tank">external tank</a>.</li></ol>\n<p>The first three phases would have required about $60 million in 1980s dollars, not including launch costs.\n</p><p>Other options for launching TRS were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_III" title="Titan III">Titan III</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_Agena" title="Atlas Agena">Atlas Agena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_110-4"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_departure">After departure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: After departure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_4_undocking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3917" data-file-width="3917" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/220px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/330px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/440px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_4_undocking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skylab in February 1974, as SL-4 departs</div></div></div>\n<p>After a boost of 6.8 miles (10.9&#160;km) by SL-4\'s Apollo CSM before its departure in 1974, Skylab was left in a <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a> of 269 miles (433&#160;km) by 283 miles (455&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson361_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson361-124">[124]</a></sup> that was expected to last until at least the early 1980s, based on estimates of the 11-year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspot_cycle" title="Sunspot cycle">sunspot cycle</a> that began in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp|Edelson|1979|p=65_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvp|Edelson|1979|p=65-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-137">[137]</a></sup> NASA first considered as early as 1962 the potential risks of a space station reentry, but decided not to incorporate a <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorocket</a> system in Skylab due to cost and acceptable risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spent 49-ton <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> stage which had launched Skylab in 1973 remained in orbit for almost two years, and made an uncontrolled reentry on January 11, 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>  Some debris, most prominently the five heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J-2_(rocket_engine)" title="J-2 (rocket engine)">J-2</a> engines, likely survived to impact in the North Atlantic Ocean.  Although this event did not receive heavy media or public attention, it was followed closely by NASA and the Air Force, and helped emphasize the need for improved planning and public awareness for Skylab\'s eventual reentry.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_activity">Solar activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Solar activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2361" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/220px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/330px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/440px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skylab captured this view of the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p>British mathematician <a href="/wiki/Desmond_King-Hele" title="Desmond King-Hele">Desmond King-Hele</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">Royal Aircraft Establishment</a> predicted in 1973 that Skylab would de-orbit and crash to earth in 1979, sooner than NASA\'s forecast, because of increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-137">[137]</a></sup> Greater-than-expected solar activity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> heated the outer layers of Earth\'s atmosphere and increased drag on Skylab. By late 1977, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NORAD" title="NORAD">NORAD</a> also forecast a reentry in mid-1979;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp|Edelson|1979|p=65_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-harvp|Edelson|1979|p=65-136">[136]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA) scientist criticized NASA for using an inaccurate model for the second most-intense sunspot cycle in a century, and for ignoring NOAA predictions published in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The reentry of the USSR\'s nuclear powered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmos_954" title="Cosmos 954">Cosmos 954</a> in January 1978, and the resulting radioactive debris fall in northern Canada, drew more attention to Skylab\'s orbit. Although Skylab did not contain radioactive materials, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> warned NASA about the potential diplomatic repercussions of station debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Battelle_Memorial_Institute" title="Battelle Memorial Institute">Battelle Memorial Institute</a> forecast that up to 25 tons of metal debris could land in 500 pieces over an area 4,000 miles long and 1,000 miles wide. The lead-lined film vault, for example, might land intact at 400 feet per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ground controllers re-established contact with Skylab in March 1978<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> and recharged its batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-4">[4]</a></sup> Although NASA worked on plans to reboost Skylab with the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> through 1978 and the TRS was almost complete, the agency gave up in December when it became clear that the shuttle would not be ready in time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790101_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-time19790101-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> its first flight, <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a>, did not occur until April 1981. Also rejected were proposals to launch the TRS using one or two unmanned rockets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_112-5"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-112">[112]</a></sup> or to attempt to destroy the station with missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-entry_and_debris">Re-entry and debris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Re-entry and debris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_reentry_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/220px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/330px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/440px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_reentry_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Equirectangular_projection" title="Equirectangular projection">Equirectangular projection</a> relief map of the Skylab re-entry site and final orbits, as predicted by NASA</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylabfragment.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="692" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/220px-Skylabfragment.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/330px-Skylabfragment.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/440px-Skylabfragment.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylabfragment.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragment of Skylab recovered after its re-entry through Earth\'s atmosphere, on display at the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Skylab\'s demise in 1979 was an international media event, with T-shirts and hats with bullseyes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> and "Skylab Repellent" with a money-back guarantee,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrier19790708_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-carrier19790708-145">[145]</a></sup> wagering on the time and place of re-entry, and nightly news reports. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Examiner" title="San Francisco Examiner">San Francisco Examiner</a></i> offered a $10,000 prize for the first piece of Skylab delivered to its offices; the competing <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i> offered $200,000 if a subscriber suffered personal or property damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-4">[4]</a></sup> A Nebraska neighborhood painted a target so that the station would have "something to aim for", a resident said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrier19790708_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-carrier19790708-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA calculated that the odds were 1 to 152 of debris hitting any human.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The odds were 1 to 7 of debris hitting a city of 100,000 people or more, and special teams were readied to head to any country hit by debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-4">[4]</a></sup> The event caused so much panic in the Philippines that President <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> appeared on national television to reassure the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A week before re-entry, NASA forecast that it would occur between July 10 and 14, with the 12th the most likely date, and the Royal Aircraft Establishment predicted the 14th.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-137">[137]</a></sup> In the hours before the event, ground controllers adjusted Skylab\'s orientation to minimize the risk of re-entry on a populated area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-4">[4]</a></sup> They aimed the station at a spot 810 miles (1,300&#160;km) south-southeast of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, South Africa, and re-entry began at approximately 16:37 UTC, July 11, 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-3">[3]</a></sup> The Air Force provided data from a secret tracking system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindoh_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindoh-147">[147]</a></sup> The station did not burn up as fast as NASA expected. Debris landed about 300 miles (480&#160;km) east of <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, Western Australia due to a 4% calculation error,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-3">[3]</a></sup> and was found between <a href="/wiki/Esperance,_Western_Australia" title="Esperance, Western Australia">Esperance, Western Australia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rawlinna" title="Rawlinna">Rawlinna</a>, from 31&#176; to 34&#176;S and 122&#176; to 126&#176;E, about 130&#8211;150&#160;km (81&#8211;93 miles) radius around <a href="/wiki/Balladonia,_Western_Australia" title="Balladonia, Western Australia">Balladonia, Western Australia</a>. Residents and an airline pilot saw dozens of colorful flares as large pieces broke up in the atmosphere;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> although the debris landed in the most unpopulated land on earth, the sightings caused NASA to fear human injury or property damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindoh_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-lindoh-147">[147]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shire_of_Esperance" title="Shire of Esperance">Shire of Esperance</a> fined NASA A$400 for littering;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> the fine was paid in April 2009, when radio show host Scott Barley of Highway Radio raised the funds from his morning show listeners on behalf of NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Stan Thornton found 24 pieces of Skylab at his home in Esperance, and a Philadelphia businessman flew him, his parents, and his girlfriend to San Francisco where he collected the <i>Examiner</i> prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> In a coincidence for the organizers, the annual <a href="/wiki/Miss_Universe_1979" title="Miss Universe 1979">Miss Universe</a> pageant was scheduled a few days later on July 20, 1979 in Perth, and a large piece of Skylab debris was displayed on the stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Analysis of the debris showed that the station had disintegrated 10 miles (16&#160;km) above the Earth, much lower than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the demise of Skylab, NASA focused on the reusable <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a> module, an orbital workshop that could be deployed with the Space Shuttle and returned to Earth. The next American major space station project was <a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom" title="Space Station Freedom">Space Station Freedom</a>, which was merged into the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> in 1993 and launched starting in 1998. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle-Mir" title="Shuttle-Mir">Shuttle-Mir</a> was another project and led to the US funding <a href="/wiki/Spektr" title="Spektr">Spektr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priroda" title="Priroda">Priroda</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mir_Docking_Module" title="Mir Docking Module">Mir Docking Module</a> in the 1990s.\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_reentering_space_debris" title="List of reentering space debris">List of reentering space debris</a></div>\n<h2><span id="Rockets.2C_rescue.2C_and_cancelled_missions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockets,_rescue,_and_cancelled_missions">Rockets, rescue, and cancelled missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Rockets, rescue, and cancelled missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1809" data-file-width="2276" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg/220px-Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg/330px-Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg/440px-Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_rescue_cm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>5-person Apollo Command module for the Apollo Rescue mission</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/220px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/330px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/440px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SA-209 served on standby for Skylab 4 and ASTP, and has been preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> rocket garden.</div></div></div>\n<p>There was a <a href="/wiki/Skylab_Rescue" title="Skylab Rescue">Skylab Rescue</a> mission assembled for the second manned mission to Skylab, but it was not needed. Another rescue mission was assembled for the last Skylab and was also on standby for ASTP. That launch stack might have been used for Skylab 5, what would be the fourth manned Skylab mission but this was cancelled and the SA-209 Saturn IB rocket was put on display at NASA <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historicspacecraft.com_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-historicspacecraft.com-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Launch vehicles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historicspacecraft.com_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-historicspacecraft.com-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>SA-206 (Skylab 2)</li>\n<li>SA-207 (Skylab 3)</li>\n<li>SA-208 (Skylab 4)</li>\n<li>SA-209 (Skylab Rescue, not-launched)</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_5">Skylab 5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Skylab 5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Skylab 5 would have been a short 20-day mission to conduct more scientific experiments and use the Apollo\'s Service Propulsion System engine to boost Skylab into a higher orbit. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vance_Brand" title="Vance Brand">Vance Brand</a> (commander), <a href="/wiki/William_B._Lenoir" title="William B. Lenoir">William B. Lenoir</a> (science pilot), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_Lind" title="Don Lind">Don Lind</a> (pilot) would have been the crew for this mission, with Brand and Lind being the prime crew for the Skylab Rescue flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautixskylab5_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautixskylab5-153">[153]</a></sup> Brand and Lind also trained for a mission that would have aimed Skylab for a controlled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deorbit" title="Deorbit">deorbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindoh_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-lindoh-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission would have launched in April 1974 and supported later use by the Space Shuttle, at the time in development, by boosting the station to higher orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_B">Skylab B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Skylab B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab_B" title="Skylab B">Skylab B</a></div>\n<p>In addition to the flown Skylab space station, a second flight-quality backup Skylab space station had been built during the program. NASA considered using it for a second station in May 1973 or later, to be called Skylab B (S-IVB 515), but decided against it. Launching another Skylab with another Saturn V rocket would have been very costly, and it was decided to spend this money on the development of the Space Shuttle instead. The backup is on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_mock-ups">Engineering mock-ups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Engineering mock-ups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A full-size training mock-up once used for astronaut training is located at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center</a> visitor\'s center in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>.  Another full-size training mock-up is at the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>.  Originally displayed indoors, it was subsequently stored outdoors for several years to make room for other exhibits.  To mark the 40th anniversary of the Skylab program, the Orbital Workshop portion of the trainer was restored and moved into the Davidson Center in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>  NASA transferred <a href="/wiki/Skylab_B" title="Skylab B">the backup Skylab</a> to the National Air and Space Museum in 1975. On display in the Museum\'s Space Hall since 1976, the orbital workshop has been slightly modified to permit viewers to walk through the living quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_designations">Mission designations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Mission designations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The numerical identification of the manned Skylab missions was the cause of some confusion. Originally, the unmanned launch of Skylab and the three manned missions to the station were numbered <i>SL-1</i> through <i>SL-4</i>. During the preparations for the manned missions, some documentation was created with a different scheme&#8212;<i>SLM-1</i> through <i>SLM-3</i>&#8212;for those missions only. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Pogue" title="William Pogue">William Pogue</a> credits <a href="/wiki/Pete_Conrad" title="Pete Conrad">Pete Conrad</a> with asking the Skylab program director which scheme should be used for the <a href="/wiki/Mission_patch" title="Mission patch">mission patches</a>, and the astronauts were told to use 1-2-3, not 2-3-4. By the time NASA administrators tried to reverse this decision, it was too late, as all the in-flight clothing had already been manufactured and shipped with the 1-2-3 mission patches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 100px;">Mission\n</th>\n<th Pogue">William Pogue</a>\n</td>\n<td>1973-11-16 <br/>14:01:23 UTC\n</td>\n<td>1974-02-08 <br/>15:16:53 UTC\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">84.04\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skylab_5" title="Skylab 5">Skylab 5</a>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vance_Brand" title="Vance Brand">Vance Brand</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/William_B._Lenoir" title="William B. The test had a three-man crew with Commander (Crippen), Science Pilot (Bobko), and Pilot (Thornton);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> there was a focus on medical studies and Thornton was an M.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The crew lived and worked in the pressure chamber, converted to be like Skylab, from July 26 to September 20, 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 100px;">Mission\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Emblem\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 120px;">Commander\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 120px;">Science Pilot\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 120px;">Pilot\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 175px;">Start date\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 175px;">End date\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width: 50px;">Duration (days)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Skylab_Medical_Experiment_Altitude_Test" title="Skylab Medical Experiment Altitude Test">SMEAT</a>\n</th>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMEAT_patch.jpg"><img alt="SMEAT patch.jpg" data-file-height="314" data-file-width="318" decoding="async" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/SMEAT_patch.jpg/50px-SMEAT_patch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/SMEAT_patch.jpg/75px-SMEAT_patch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/SMEAT_patch.jpg/100px-SMEAT_patch.jpg 2x" width="50"/></a></div></div>\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Crippen" title="Bob Crippen">Bob Crippen</a>\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karol_Bobko" title="Karol Bobko">Karol Bobko</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/William_E._Thornton" title="William E. Thornton">William Thornton</a>\n</td>\n<td>July 26, 1972\n</td>\n<td>September 20, 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>56-days\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_cost">Program cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Program cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>From 1966 to 1974, the Skylab program cost a total of $2.2&#160;billion, equivalent to $10&#160;billion in 2010 dollars. As its three three-man crews spent 510 total man-days in space, each man-day cost approximately $20&#160;million, compared to $7.5&#160;million for the International Space Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-162">[162]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span Missions.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image"
